<p>The <b>European storm petrel</b>, <b>British storm petrel</b>, or just <b>storm petrel</b> (<i>Hydrobates pelagicus</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">seabird</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_storm_petrel" title="Northern storm petrel">northern storm petrel</a> <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a>, Hydrobatidae. It is the <a href="/wiki/Monotypic_taxon" title="Monotypic taxon">only member</a> of the genus <i>Hydrobates</i>. The small, square-tailed bird is entirely black except for a broad, white rump and a white band on the under wings, and it has a fluttering, bat-like flight. The large majority of the population breeds on islands off the coasts of Europe, with the greatest numbers in the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>, United Kingdom, Ireland, and Iceland. The Mediterranean population is a separate subspecies, but is inseparable at sea from its Atlantic relatives; its strongholds are <a href="/wiki/Filfla" title="Filfla">Filfla</a> Island (Malta), <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Balearic_Islands" title="Balearic Islands">Balearic Islands</a>.
</p><p>The storm petrel nests in crevices and burrows, sometimes shared with other seabirds or rabbits, and lays a single white egg, usually on bare soil. The adults share the lengthy <a href="/wiki/Egg_incubation" title="Egg incubation">incubation</a> and both feed the chick, which is not normally brooded after the first week. This bird is strongly <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migratory</a>, spending the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a> winter mainly off the coasts of South Africa and Namibia, with some birds stopping in the seas adjoining West Africa, and a few remaining near their Mediterranean breeding islands. This petrel is strictly oceanic outside the breeding season. It feeds on small fish, squid, and <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a>, while pattering on the sea's surface, and can find oily edible items by smell. The food is converted in the bird's stomach to an oily orange liquid, which is regurgitated when the chick is fed. Although usually silent at sea, the storm petrel has a chattering call given by both members of a pair in their courtship flight, and the male has a purring song given from the breeding chamber.
</p><p>The storm petrel cannot survive on islands where land mammals such as rats and cats have been introduced, and it suffers natural predation from <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gulls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skua" title="Skua">skuas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owls</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Falcon" title="Falcon">falcons</a>. Although the population may be declining slightly, this petrel is classified by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> as being of <a href="/wiki/Least_concern" class="mw-redirect" title="Least concern">least concern</a> due to its high total numbers. Its presence in rough weather at sea has led to various mariners' superstitions, and by analogy, to its use as a symbol by revolutionary and <a href="/wiki/Anarchy" title="Anarchy">anarchist</a> groups.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_storm_petrel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Storm_petrel" title="Storm petrel">storm petrels</a>, Hydrobatidae, are one of the four major <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">families</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Procellariiformes" title="Procellariiformes">Procellariiformes</a> or "tubenoses", an <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a> of <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">seabirds</a> that also includes the <a href="/wiki/Albatross" title="Albatross">albatrosses</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Procellariidae" title="Procellariidae">Procellariidae</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Diving_petrel" title="Diving petrel">diving petrels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ioc_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ioc-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The family is an ancient group of small species that is thought to have <a href="/wiki/Divergent_evolution" title="Divergent evolution">diverged</a> early from the rest of the tubenoses; the supporting fossil record is poor, with specimens from California dating back only to the <a href="/wiki/Late_Miocene" title="Late Miocene">Late Miocene</a> (11.6–5.3 million years ago). The Hydrobatidae are often divided into two subfamilies, the mainly <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a> Oceanitinae and the northern Hydrobatinae;<sup id="cite_ref-hbwfam_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbwfam-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome_b" title="Cytochrome b">cytochrome <i>b</i></a> <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid_sequence" title="Nucleic acid sequence">DNA sequence</a> analysis suggests that these might be full families.<sup id="cite_ref-Nunn1998_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nunn1998-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The European storm petrel is the only member of the genus <i>Hydrobates</i>, the remainder of the Hydrobatinae being placed in <i><a href="/wiki/Oceanodroma" title="Oceanodroma">Oceanodroma</a></i>, although the <a href="/wiki/Least_storm_petrel" title="Least storm petrel">least storm petrel</a> is sometimes separated as the sole member of <i>Halocyptena</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-hbwfam_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbwfam-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The relationships within the Hydrobatinae are complex and uncertain, and all the members of the subfamily could be subsumed into an enlarged <i>Hydrobates</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Christidis_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christidis-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The storm petrel was first described by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in his landmark 1758 <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">10th edition of <i>Systema Naturae</i></a> as <i>Procellaria pelagica</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Linn_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Linn-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> It was moved to the genus <i>Hydrobates</i> by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Boie" title="Friedrich Boie">Friedrich Boie</a> in 1822.<sup id="cite_ref-Ernst1979_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst1979-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Boie1822_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boie1822-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> "Petrel", first recorded in 1602, is a corruption of <i>pitteral</i>, referring to the bird's pitter-pattering across the water. The suggestion that the word refers to <a href="/wiki/Saint_Peter" title="Saint Peter">St Peter</a>'s walking on the waves is a later invention. "Storm" arises from seamen's association of this bird with bad weather.<sup id="cite_ref-lockwood115_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lockwood115-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In English, the name of the species was written as "storm<b>y</b> petrel" by some 19th-century authors.<sup id="cite_ref-eg_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eg-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">scientific name</a> <i>hydrobates</i> derives from <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/hydro-" class="extiw" title="wikt:hydro-">hydro-</a>", from <i>hydōr</i> "water", and <i>batēs</i> "walker",<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and <i>pelagicus</i> from <i>pelagikos</i> "pelagic, of the (open) sea", from <i>pelagos</i> "sea, open sea, high sea".<sup id="cite_ref-job393_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-job393-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-job404_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-job404-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> There are two recognised <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a>, the North Atlantic <a href="/wiki/Subspecies#Nominotypical_subspecies_and_subspecies_autonyms" title="Subspecies">nominate subspecies</a>, <i>H. p. pelagicus</i> (Linnaeus, 1758), and the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a> <i>H. p. melitensis</i> (<a href="/wiki/Antonio_Schembri_(ornithologist)" title="Antonio Schembri (ornithologist)">Schembri</a>, 1843).<sup id="cite_ref-ioc_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ioc-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Although there is some <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genetic</a> support for classifying the southern form as a separate species,<sup id="cite_ref-gagnon_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gagnon-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphology</a> is not considered sufficiently different from that of the nominate subspecies to justify a split.<sup id="cite_ref-hbwfam_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbwfam-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_storm_petrel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hydrobates_pelagicus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Hydrobates_pelagicus.jpg/220px-Hydrobates_pelagicus.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Hydrobates_pelagicus.jpg/330px-Hydrobates_pelagicus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Hydrobates_pelagicus.jpg/440px-Hydrobates_pelagicus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hydrobates_pelagicus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Storm petrels cannot walk on land; they shuffle on their <a href="/wiki/Tarsus_(skeleton)" title="Tarsus (skeleton)">tarsi</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oceanite.tempete.jogo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Oceanite.tempete.jogo.jpg/220px-Oceanite.tempete.jogo.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4a/Oceanite.tempete.jogo.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="275" data-file-height="212" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oceanite.tempete.jogo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Old illustrations, such as this one by <a href="/wiki/John_Gould" title="John Gould">John Gould</a>, were painted from skins, and showed petrels in improbable standing positions.<sup id="cite_ref-Coward_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coward-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The storm petrel is a small bird, 14–18&#160;cm (5.5–7.1&#160;in) in length with a 36–39&#160;cm (14–15&#160;in) wingspan.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> It weighs 20–38&#160;g (0.71–1.34&#160;oz), with an average of 28&#160;g (0.99&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-debize_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-debize-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> It is square-tailed and has all-black <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a> except for a snow-white rump that extends to the sides of the tail base and a broad white band on the under wings. Juveniles in fresh plumage can also show a narrow white bar on the upper wing. The plumage becomes dark brown rather than black as it becomes worn.<sup id="cite_ref-Mullarney_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mullarney-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> No obvious differences between the sexes are seen, although in the Mediterranean subspecies, at least, most captured birds can be sexed using a formula which involves multiplying the wing length by the length of the white rump band; females are slightly larger and have a longer white rump than males.<sup id="cite_ref-baraja_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baraja-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The Mediterranean subspecies has longer wings and a heavier bill on average than the nominate form, but neither sex nor subspecies can be determined by observation at sea.<sup id="cite_ref-vand_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vand-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Moulting" title="Moulting">Moult</a> is prolonged in all tubenoses, since they must maintain an ability to fly. Northern populations start replacing their plumage after those further south, reflecting the later start to their breeding season. Birds in a Welsh colony commenced moulting in early August, while populations in northern Spain and the Balearics started in early July and mid-June, respectively. Breeding birds moult later than non-breeders.<sup id="cite_ref-moult_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moult-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The storm petrel's large nasal <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_bulb" title="Olfactory bulb">olfactory bulbs</a> facilitate a keen sense of smell (unlike most birds),<sup id="cite_ref-Enticott196_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Enticott196-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> and the birds have a distinctive musty aroma which can help researchers locate breeding colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-bblamp_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bblamp-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Individual petrels recognise their own body scent and can use it to locate their nests in the dark.<sup id="cite_ref-deleon_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deleon-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Their flight is weak-looking and resembles that of a bat, with fluttering interspersed with short glides. When feeding, the birds hang with raised wings and patter on the surface with their feet, but unlike <a href="/wiki/Wilson%27s_storm_petrel" title="Wilson&#39;s storm petrel">Wilson's storm petrel</a>, do not look as if they are walking on the water. Birds sometimes settle on the sea.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Like other petrels, the European storm petrel cannot walk properly on land, but shuffles on its <a href="/wiki/Tarsus_(skeleton)" title="Tarsus (skeleton)">tarsi</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Soper_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soper-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> once there is enough room, the bird flaps its wings to support itself on its toes.<sup id="cite_ref-hume110_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hume110-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The European storm petrel can be distinguished from related <a href="/wiki/Western_Palaearctic" title="Western Palaearctic">Western Palaearctic</a> species by the white bar on its under wing and its distinctive fluttering flight. Compared to <a href="/wiki/Leach%27s_storm_petrel" title="Leach&#39;s storm petrel">Leach's storm petrel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Band-rumped_storm_petrel" title="Band-rumped storm petrel">band-rumped storm petrel</a>, and the recently described <a href="/wiki/Monteiro%27s_storm_petrel" title="Monteiro&#39;s storm petrel">Monteiro's storm petrel</a>, it is also smaller, darker, and shorter-winged, and has a square tail. Wilson's storm petrel lacks an under wing bar, and has long legs with the feet visible beyond the tail.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Voice">Voice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_storm_petrel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Voice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="125.42800453515" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Storm_Petrel_(Hydrobates_pelagicus)_(W1CDR0001395_BD7).ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/3/3c/Storm_Petrel_%28Hydrobates_pelagicus%29_%28W1CDR0001395_BD7%29.ogg/Storm_Petrel_%28Hydrobates_pelagicus%29_%28W1CDR0001395_BD7%29.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="135144" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3c/Storm_Petrel_%28Hydrobates_pelagicus%29_%28W1CDR0001395_BD7%29.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (235 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="234778" /></audio></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Storm_Petrel_(Hydrobates_pelagicus)_(W1CDR0001395_BD7).ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Storm petrel churring calls</div></div></div>
<p>In its display flight, the storm petrel gives a call consisting of eight or more repetitions of a fast <i>ter-CHICK</i> sounds ending in a <a href="/wiki/Trill_(music)" title="Trill (music)">trill</a> (rapid alternation of notes). This chattering, staccato call is highly variable in pitch, stress, and length.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-debize_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-debize-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Both sexes make the call, which is used as an advertisement for a mate, for pair recognition, and in the nuptial flight. The details of the vocalisation vary geographically, including between the Atlantic and Mediterranean populations, and birds recognise calls from their own breeding area.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_18-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bretagnolle_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bretagnolle-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The chatter-call of the Mediterranean subspecies is distinctive. It has the first two notes running into each other, and the final element is sometimes doubled.<sup id="cite_ref-vand_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vand-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The storm petrel is usually silent at sea, but sometimes gives the chattering call.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_18-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> A purring song <i>arrr-r-r-r-r-r-r</i><span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... ending with a sharp <i>chikka</i> is given in the burrow only by the male;<sup id="cite_ref-debize_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-debize-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bretagnolle_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bretagnolle-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> it was described by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Oldham_(naturalist)" title="Charles Oldham (naturalist)">Charles Oldham</a> as "like a fairy being sick".<sup id="cite_ref-bbDavis1_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbDavis1-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Other vocalisations include a fast <i>wick-wick-wick</i>, sometimes given in flight, and an <i>up-CHERRK</i> alarm which resembles the chatter-call.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_18-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Chicks give a whistling <i>pee-pee-pee</i> call when being fed, and a faster version of this vocalisation is used by adults and young to signal distress.<sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_storm_petrel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%22Ringing%22_a_Storm_Petrel_by_Cape_Wrath_-_geograph.org.uk_-_753403.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/%22Ringing%22_a_Storm_Petrel_by_Cape_Wrath_-_geograph.org.uk_-_753403.jpg/220px-%22Ringing%22_a_Storm_Petrel_by_Cape_Wrath_-_geograph.org.uk_-_753403.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/%22Ringing%22_a_Storm_Petrel_by_Cape_Wrath_-_geograph.org.uk_-_753403.jpg/330px-%22Ringing%22_a_Storm_Petrel_by_Cape_Wrath_-_geograph.org.uk_-_753403.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/%22Ringing%22_a_Storm_Petrel_by_Cape_Wrath_-_geograph.org.uk_-_753403.jpg/440px-%22Ringing%22_a_Storm_Petrel_by_Cape_Wrath_-_geograph.org.uk_-_753403.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="471" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%22Ringing%22_a_Storm_Petrel_by_Cape_Wrath_-_geograph.org.uk_-_753403.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bird_ringing" title="Bird ringing">Ringing</a> at <a href="/wiki/Cape_Wrath" title="Cape Wrath">Cape Wrath</a></div></div></div>
<p>Storm petrels breed only in the Western Palaearctic on islands off the Atlantic and Mediterranean coasts of Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_18-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The largest colonies are in the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a> (150,000–400,000 pairs), United Kingdom (20,000–150,000), Ireland (50,000–100,000), and Iceland (50,000–100,000), with smaller breeding areas off Norway, Malta, Spain, the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a>, Italy, France, and Greece.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The strongholds of the <i>H. p. melitensis</i> subspecies are the islands of <a href="/wiki/Filfla" title="Filfla">Filfla</a> (Malta), <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Balearic_Islands" title="Balearic Islands">Balearic Islands</a>, with smaller sites elsewhere in the Mediterranean. This latter form also breeds in North Africa; definitely in Tunisia, probably in Algeria and possibly in Morocco.<sup id="cite_ref-debize_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-debize-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Because of its nocturnal habits and the problems of accessing some of the small islands on which it breeds, the distribution is poorly known. A colony was discovered as recently as 2009, on <a href="/wiki/Lampedusa" title="Lampedusa">Lampedusa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bblamp_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bblamp-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The storm petrel has been recorded as a vagrant in several European countries as far east as the Ukraine, in the <a href="/wiki/Guinea_(region)" title="Guinea (region)">Guinea region</a> of West Africa, and in Turkey, Israel, Lebanon,<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> and the US.<sup id="cite_ref-mcneil_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcneil-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Although no North American records were reported for more than 30 years after the first in 1970, this bird has been more or less annual in small numbers since 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-howell_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-howell-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The storm petrel breeds on exposed and usually uninhabited islands, which it visits only at night. It otherwise frequents mid-depth waters away from the coastal zone, but not over the ocean deeps. In the breeding season, it is mainly found between the 10–25<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>°C July <a href="/wiki/Isotherm_(contour_line)" class="mw-redirect" title="Isotherm (contour line)">isotherms</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_18-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In Europe, it is rarely seen from land except in autumn storms.<sup id="cite_ref-Blomdahl_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blomdahl-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The storm petrel is <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migratory</a>, spending the Northern Hemisphere winter mainly in cool waters off the coasts of South Africa and Namibia, south to <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a> 38°S and east to <a href="/wiki/KwaZulu-Natal" title="KwaZulu-Natal">KwaZulu-Natal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_18-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SASOL_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SASOL-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Some birds stay north of the equator in the seas adjoining Mauritania and <a href="/wiki/R%C3%ADo_de_Oro" title="Río de Oro">Rio de Oro</a>, and a few remain near the breeding islands, especially in the Mediterranean. It is strictly oceanic outside the breeding season,<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_18-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hbwonlinehydrobates_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbwonlinehydrobates-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> although it is described as regularly seen from land in West Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Barlow_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barlow-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Young birds do not return to the breeding colonies until their second or third year. Birds mostly head south from the breeding islands between September and November, reaching West Africa by mid-November and the south Atlantic by the end of the year. The return passage starts in April, with late records from the tropics and further south probably representing subadult birds that will not breed that year.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_18-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour">Behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_storm_petrel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_storm_petrel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The storm petrel is sexually mature at age 4–5 years, with the Mediterranean subspecies typically breeding a year earlier than the Atlantic form. Breeding happens in <a href="/wiki/Colony_(biology)" title="Colony (biology)">colonies</a> and normally begins in late May or June.<sup id="cite_ref-bblamp_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bblamp-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hbwonlinehydrobates_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbwonlinehydrobates-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Pairs have a repeated nocturnal display flight in which the male chases the female, the chase being accompanied by flight calls. Some near-adult birds may pair up and occupy a hole at the same time, prior to breeding in the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_18-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Storm petrels normally nest in crevices between or under rocks, or burrow in the soil.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_18-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> When they make their own tunnels, they loosen the earth with their bills and kick out the debris with their feet. The birds less commonly nest in walls, under buildings, or down rabbit burrows. Disused or occupied burrows of <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_puffin" title="Atlantic puffin">Atlantic puffins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manx_shearwater" title="Manx shearwater">Manx shearwaters</a> are sometimes used, and the petrel pair may share a common entrance with those seabirds, rabbits, or other pairs of its own species. Where other occupants are present, the petrels dig a side burrow or use an existing low-roofed tunnel which the larger birds or rabbits cannot easily enter. Even so, puffins and shearwaters sometimes access and destroy nests, and adult petrels may be killed by their larger neighbours.<sup id="cite_ref-bbDavis1_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbDavis1-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Human-made plastic nesting tubes are readily used, and may provide protection against predators.<sup id="cite_ref-debize_19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-debize-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bolton_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bolton-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Birds usually mate for life and use the same hole every year.<sup id="cite_ref-bbDavis1_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbDavis1-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The nest tunnel is 10–300&#160;cm (3.9–118.1&#160;in) long and 5–8&#160;cm (2.0–3.1&#160;in) across, with a slightly narrower entrance. The nest chamber is typically unlined, although pairs may bring in some grass, <a href="/wiki/Bracken" title="Bracken">bracken</a>, or seaweed. Although the storm petrel is generally not territorial when breeding, a pair defends the nest chamber itself after the eggs have been laid.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_18-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Clutch_(eggs)" title="Clutch (eggs)">clutch</a> is a single egg, usually pure white, sometimes with some reddish-brown spots that soon disappear.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_18-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The average size of the egg is 28&#160;mm ×&#160;21&#160;mm (1.10&#160;in ×&#160;0.83&#160;in), and it weighs 6.8&#160;g (0.24&#160;oz), of which 6% is shell.<sup id="cite_ref-bto_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bto-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> If an egg is lost early enough, a replacement may be laid on rare occasions. This is very unusual for tubenoses.<sup id="cite_ref-ming_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ming-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The eggs are <a href="/wiki/Egg_incubation" title="Egg incubation">incubated</a> by both parents for 38–50<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>days, the longer periods arising when the eggs have become chilled through adult absence.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_18-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> One adult typically spends three days at a time on the egg while its partner feeds at sea.<sup id="cite_ref-bbDavis1_30-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbDavis1-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The chicks are <a href="/wiki/Altricial" class="mw-redirect" title="Altricial">altricial</a> and covered with silver-grey down, and are fed by both parents with their regurgitated oily stomach contents.<sup id="cite_ref-Coward_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coward-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hbwonlinehydrobates_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbwonlinehydrobates-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bto_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bto-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The adults do not normally stay with the chick after the first week, visiting only to bring food.<sup id="cite_ref-bbDavis2_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbDavis2-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> After about 50<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>days, the chicks are fed less regularly, sometimes with gaps of several days, and the parents may stop visiting completely shortly before the chick leaves the nest.<sup id="cite_ref-bbDavis2_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbDavis2-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The chicks <a href="/wiki/Fledge" title="Fledge">fledge</a> about 56–86<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>days after hatching,<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_18-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> and receive no parental support after leaving the nest hole.<sup id="cite_ref-hume94_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hume94-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Tubenoses have smaller egg clutches and much longer and more variable incubation and fledging times than <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">passerines</a> with similarly sized eggs, resembling <a href="/wiki/Swift" title="Swift">swifts</a> in these developmental factors. Tubenoses and swifts have generally secure nest sites, but their food sources are unreliable, whereas passerines are vulnerable in the nest but food is usually plentiful.<sup id="cite_ref-lacklack_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lacklack-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Boersma_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boersma-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In the particular case of the storm petrel, it has a body temperature perhaps 3<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>°C lower than other small birds, and this may also contribute to the lengthy incubation.<sup id="cite_ref-hume94_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hume94-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The adult petrel's annual death rate is 12–13%,<sup id="cite_ref-hbwonlinehydrobates_36-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbwonlinehydrobates-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> and the typical life span is 11<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>years. Longevity records established from <a href="/wiki/Bird_ringing" title="Bird ringing">bird ringing</a> recoveries include a bird aged 31<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>years<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>11 months<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>9 days,<sup id="cite_ref-bto_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bto-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> and another aged more than 33<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>years.<sup id="cite_ref-euring_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-euring-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_storm_petrel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Velella_Bae_an_Anaon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Velella_Bae_an_Anaon.jpg/170px-Velella_Bae_an_Anaon.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="185" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Velella_Bae_an_Anaon.jpg/255px-Velella_Bae_an_Anaon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Velella_Bae_an_Anaon.jpg/340px-Velella_Bae_an_Anaon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1086" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Velella_Bae_an_Anaon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Velella" title="Velella">by-the-wind sailor</a> is a small <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a> eaten by storm petrels.<sup id="cite_ref-Elbee_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elbee-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The storm petrel normally flies within 10&#160;m (33&#160;ft) of the water surface and typically feeds by picking items off the sea as it patters over the surface.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_18-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Birds have been observed diving for food to a depth of not more than 0.5&#160;m (20&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-bblflood_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bblflood-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-griffiths_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-griffiths-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> and it is claimed, using indirect measurements, that the Mediterranean subspecies reaches depths of up to 5&#160;m (16&#160;ft)).<sup id="cite_ref-albores_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-albores-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> A bird may range up to 200&#160;km (120&#160;mi) over the course of two or three days in search of food.<sup id="cite_ref-parkin_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parkin-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Although the bird usually feeds during the day, in the breeding season petrels will often feed at night close to the shore.<sup id="cite_ref-Elbee_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elbee-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-poot_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-poot-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The typical prey consists of surface organisms such as small fish, <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a>. The storm petrel will also eat offal and oily food, often located by smell, and will follow ships.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_18-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-humerspb_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-humerspb-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> In the Atlantic, more than half the food items are <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a> and the fish caught include small <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_herring" title="Atlantic herring">herring</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_sprat" title="European sprat">sprats</a>; whale carcasses are scavenged where available. During digestion, the plankton is quickly converted to an oily orange liquid that owes its colour to <a href="/wiki/Carotenoid" title="Carotenoid">carotenoids</a>. Larger prey items take longer to digest.<sup id="cite_ref-Elbee_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elbee-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The oil, rich in <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_A" title="Vitamin A">vitamin A</a>, is produced by a large gland in the stomach.<sup id="cite_ref-Karleskint_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karleskint-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The Mediterranean subspecies' diet is mainly fish, particularly <a href="/wiki/Gymnammodytes_cicerelus" title="Gymnammodytes cicerelus">Mediterranean sand eels</a>. Petrels also catch <a href="/wiki/Mysida" title="Mysida">opossum shrimps</a> from waters close to the colony.<sup id="cite_ref-debize_19-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-debize-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bluefin_tuna" title="Bluefin tuna">Bluefin tuna</a> farms are exploited on the Maltese island of Filfla; birds from the large local colony feed on the unwashed food fed to the farmed tuna, a mixture of fish, squid and prawns which produces a sizeable oily slick.<sup id="cite_ref-borg_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-borg-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Small numbers of insects are caught near breeding colonies, and some plant material, including <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">angiosperm</a> seeds and <a href="/wiki/Sorrel" title="Sorrel">sorrel</a>, has been found in the stomach contents.<sup id="cite_ref-Coward_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coward-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Elbee_46-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elbee-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> A study on Leach's storm petrel, which consumes similar items, showed that the petrels were snipping pieces off plant leaves in flight, but it could not be confirmed that this was in the course of catching insects.<sup id="cite_ref-BBainslie_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBainslie-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Nasal glands remove excess salt from seawater consumed by the petrel as a concentrated solution excreted through the nostrils.<sup id="cite_ref-Karleskint_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karleskint-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Petrels can be attracted to boats with "<a href="/wiki/Chumming" title="Chumming">chum</a>", a malodorous mixture typically containing fish heads, bones and offal, with added fish oil and <a href="/wiki/Popcorn" title="Popcorn">popcorn</a> to aid flotation. An apparently empty ocean will soon fill with hundreds of birds attracted by the smell. The attraction of the fishy odour is sometimes enhanced by the addition of <a href="/wiki/Dimethyl_sulfide" title="Dimethyl sulfide">dimethylsulphide</a> (DMS) a chemical also naturally produced by some <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">planktonic</a> organisms, although there are doubts about the safety of this possible <a href="/wiki/Carcinogen" title="Carcinogen">carcinogen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bblflood_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bblflood-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbfloodbw_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbfloodbw-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Predators_and_parasites">Predators and parasites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_storm_petrel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Predators and parasites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eleonorenfalke1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Eleonorenfalke1.jpg/220px-Eleonorenfalke1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Eleonorenfalke1.jpg/330px-Eleonorenfalke1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Eleonorenfalke1.jpg/440px-Eleonorenfalke1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="769" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eleonorenfalke1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Eleanora%27s_falcon" class="mw-redirect" title="Eleanora&#39;s falcon">Eleanora's falcon</a> is a local predator on some Mediterranean islands.</div></div></div>
<p>Adults and young are vulnerable to predation at the breeding colonies, their only defence being to spit oil.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Coward_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coward-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Petrels cannot breed on islands where rats have been introduced, and feral cats frequently kill these birds on <a href="/wiki/Foula" title="Foula">Foula</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Shetland_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Shetland Islands">Shetland Islands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cats_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cats-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_mink" title="American mink">American mink</a>, a non-native species in Europe, is a strong swimmer, and can colonise islands up to 2&#160;km (2,200&#160;yd) from the mainland.<sup id="cite_ref-ratcliffe_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ratcliffe-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Natural predators of petrels and other seabirds include skuas and large gulls. The <a href="/wiki/Yellow-legged_gull" title="Yellow-legged gull">yellow-legged gull</a> is a particular problem in the Mediterranean, and <a href="/wiki/Great_skua" title="Great skua">great skuas</a> were estimated to kill 7,500 petrels a year on <a href="/wiki/St_Kilda,_Scotland" title="St Kilda, Scotland">St Kilda</a>, an unsustainable number.<sup id="cite_ref-debize_19-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-debize-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-parkin_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parkin-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Great_black-backed_gull" title="Great black-backed gull">great black-backed gulls</a> on the Atlantic islands specialise in taking seabird chicks at night,<sup id="cite_ref-bbDavis2_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbDavis2-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcons</a> hunt adults at sea.<sup id="cite_ref-Soper_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soper-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Localised predators include <a href="/wiki/Eleonora%27s_falcon" title="Eleonora&#39;s falcon">Eleonora's falcon</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Columbretes_Islands" title="Columbretes Islands">Columbretes Islands</a> and the nocturnal <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owl</a> on the Balearics; a few owls can wipe out a colony.<sup id="cite_ref-debize_19-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-debize-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Little_owl" title="Little owl">little owl</a> is also a predator of both adults and young where it occurs.<sup id="cite_ref-bbDavis2_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbDavis2-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Feather_mite" title="Feather mite">Feather mites</a> of at least two species have been found on the storm petrel, with <i><a href="/wiki/Halipeurus_pelagicus" title="Halipeurus pelagicus">Halipeurus pelagicus</a></i> occurring at much higher densities than <i><a href="/wiki/Philoceanus_robertsi" title="Philoceanus robertsi">Philoceanus robertsi</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-louse_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-louse-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Flea" title="Flea">flea</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Xenopsylla_gratiosa" title="Xenopsylla gratiosa">Xenopsylla gratiosa</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Dermanyssidae" title="Dermanyssidae">dermanyssid</a> <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mites</a> are commonly found, with lower numbers of <a href="/wiki/Tick" title="Tick">ticks</a>. These blood-sucking parasites slow the growth rate of nestlings and may affect their survival rate.<sup id="cite_ref-merinoetal_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merinoetal-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Storm petrels seem to be largely free of <a href="/wiki/Haematozoa" title="Haematozoa">blood parasites</a>, even when in close proximity to carrier species such as the yellow-legged gull.<sup id="cite_ref-merino_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merino-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-quill_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-quill-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> It has been suggested that seabird species with long incubation periods and long lives have well-developed immune systems that prevent serious blood parasitism.<sup id="cite_ref-espartza_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-espartza-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status">Status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_storm_petrel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The European population of the storm petrel has been estimated at 430,000–510,000 breeding pairs or 1,290,000–1,530,000 individual birds and makes up 95% of the world total numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-hbwfam_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbwfam-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The population estimate includes 11,000 to 16,000 breeding pairs of the Mediterranean subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-debize_19-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-debize-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> It is very difficult to accurately determine storm petrel populations. The main method used is listening for responses to playback calls at burrow entrances, but infra-red filming may also be an option.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although this species' population now appears to be declining, the decrease is not rapid or large enough to trigger conservation vulnerability criteria. Given its high numbers, this petrel is therefore classified by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> as being of <a href="/wiki/Least_concern" class="mw-redirect" title="Least concern">least concern</a>. The perceived decline may be due increased predation from gulls, skuas and introduced mammals.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Eradication of rats protects seabird colonies and may enable recolonisation of islands cleared of rodents.<sup id="cite_ref-ratcliffe_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ratcliffe-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Predation of cave-nesting petrels in the Balearics by yellow-legged gulls is restricted to relatively few individual gulls specialising in this prey item;<sup id="cite_ref-oro_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oro-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> this means the problems can be controlled by selective culling and the provision of plastic nest boxes.<sup id="cite_ref-yesou_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yesou-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Because it feeds in flight, the storm petrel is less affected by <a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">oil pollution</a> than other seabirds, and may be able to use its good sense of smell to avoid slicks, although a large spill near a breeding colony could have serious consequences.<sup id="cite_ref-hbwfam_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbwfam-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_storm_petrel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: In culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mother_Carey_and_her_chickens_by_J_G_Keulemans_1877_(frame_removed).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Mother_Carey_and_her_chickens_by_J_G_Keulemans_1877_%28frame_removed%29.jpg/220px-Mother_Carey_and_her_chickens_by_J_G_Keulemans_1877_%28frame_removed%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Mother_Carey_and_her_chickens_by_J_G_Keulemans_1877_%28frame_removed%29.jpg/330px-Mother_Carey_and_her_chickens_by_J_G_Keulemans_1877_%28frame_removed%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Mother_Carey_and_her_chickens_by_J_G_Keulemans_1877_%28frame_removed%29.jpg/440px-Mother_Carey_and_her_chickens_by_J_G_Keulemans_1877_%28frame_removed%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="964" data-file-height="708" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mother_Carey_and_her_chickens_by_J_G_Keulemans_1877_(frame_removed).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<a href="/wiki/Mother_Carey" title="Mother Carey">Mother Carey</a> and her chickens". Lithograph by <a href="/wiki/J._G._Keulemans" class="mw-redirect" title="J. G. Keulemans">J. G. Keulemans</a>, 1877.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harper%27s_New_Monthly_Magazine_Volume_104_December_1901_to_May_1902_(1902)_(14780164871).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Harper%27s_New_Monthly_Magazine_Volume_104_December_1901_to_May_1902_%281902%29_%2814780164871%29.jpg/220px-Harper%27s_New_Monthly_Magazine_Volume_104_December_1901_to_May_1902_%281902%29_%2814780164871%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="372" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Harper%27s_New_Monthly_Magazine_Volume_104_December_1901_to_May_1902_%281902%29_%2814780164871%29.jpg/330px-Harper%27s_New_Monthly_Magazine_Volume_104_December_1901_to_May_1902_%281902%29_%2814780164871%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Harper%27s_New_Monthly_Magazine_Volume_104_December_1901_to_May_1902_%281902%29_%2814780164871%29.jpg/440px-Harper%27s_New_Monthly_Magazine_Volume_104_December_1901_to_May_1902_%281902%29_%2814780164871%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2485" data-file-height="4201" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harper%27s_New_Monthly_Magazine_Volume_104_December_1901_to_May_1902_(1902)_(14780164871).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mother Carey. Illustration by <a href="/wiki/Howard_Pyle" title="Howard Pyle">Howard Pyle</a>, 1902.</div></div></div>
<p>Its association with storms makes the storm petrel a bird of bad omen to mariners; they are said to either foretell or cause bad weather.<sup id="cite_ref-Albatross_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Albatross-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> A more prosaic explanation of their appearance in rough weather is that, like most oceanic seabirds, they rely on the winds to support them in flight and just sit on the water surface when becalmed.<sup id="cite_ref-Fisher_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fisher-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The birds were sometimes thought to be the souls of perished sailors,<sup id="cite_ref-Cocker_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cocker-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> and killing a petrel was believed to bring bad luck.<sup id="cite_ref-Albatross_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Albatross-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cocker_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cocker-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The petrel's reputation led to the old name of <i>witch</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-lockwood115_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lockwood115-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> although the commonest of the folk names is <a href="/wiki/Mother_Carey" title="Mother Carey">Mother Carey</a>'s chicken, a name also used for storm petrels in general in the UK and North America from at least 1767.<sup id="cite_ref-OED_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OED-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> This is believed to be a corruption of <i>mater cara</i> (<i>dear mother</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Mary_(mother_of_Jesus)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary (mother of Jesus)">Virgin Mary</a>), a reference to the supernatural Mother Carey, or a superstitious modification of an earlier <i>Mother Mary's chicken</i> to reduce the potency of the religious name.<sup id="cite_ref-Cocker_69-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cocker-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The Mother Carey character appears a number of times in literature. In the <a href="/wiki/C._Fox_Smith" title="C. Fox Smith">Cicely Fox Smith</a> poem "Mother Carey", she calls old sailors to return to the sea,<sup id="cite_ref-FoxSmith_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FoxSmith-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> but in <a href="/wiki/John_Masefield" title="John Masefield">John Masefield</a>'s poem of the same name she is seen as a wrecker of ships.<sup id="cite_ref-Masefield_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Masefield-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> She appears as a fairy in <a href="/wiki/Charles_Kingsley" title="Charles Kingsley">Charles Kingsley</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Water-Babies,_A_Fairy_Tale_for_a_Land_Baby" title="The Water-Babies, A Fairy Tale for a Land Baby">The Water Babies</a></i> and is depicted in one of <a href="/wiki/Jessie_Willcox_Smith" title="Jessie Willcox Smith">Jessie Willcox Smith</a>'s illustrations for the book.<sup id="cite_ref-Kingsley_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kingsley-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The association of the storm petrel with turbulent weather has led to its use as a metaphor for revolutionary views,<sup id="cite_ref-ziolkowski111_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ziolkowski111-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Epithet" title="Epithet">epithet</a> "stormy petrel" being applied by various authors to characters as disparate as <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman</a> <a href="/wiki/Tribune" title="Tribune">tribune</a> <a href="/wiki/Publius_Clodius_Pulcher" title="Publius Clodius Pulcher">Publius Clodius Pulcher</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Frost1909_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frost1909-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian">Presbyterian</a> minister in the early Carolinas,<sup id="cite_ref-Lynah1934_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lynah1934-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> an Afghan governor,<sup id="cite_ref-Grey1929_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grey1929-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> or an <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a> politician.<sup id="cite_ref-Jacobson1925_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobson1925-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
A 1901 poem by Russian writer <a href="/wiki/Maxim_Gorky" title="Maxim Gorky">Maxim Gorky</a> is invariably titled in English as "<a href="/wiki/The_Song_of_the_Stormy_Petrel" title="The Song of the Stormy Petrel">The Song of the Stormy Petrel</a>", although that may not be a perfectly accurate translation of the Russian title "Песня о Буревестнике", because "Буревестник" (the name of the bird in Russian) translates to the English general term "storm bird".<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> The poem was called "the battle anthem of the revolution", and earned Gorky the nickname "The Storm Petrel of the Revolution".<sup id="cite_ref-miro_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miro-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Various <a href="/wiki/Insurrectionary_anarchism" title="Insurrectionary anarchism">revolutionary anarchist</a> groups adopted the bird's name, either as a group identifier, as in the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Christie_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christie-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> or for their publications. <i>The Stormy Petrel</i> (<i>Burevestnik</i>) was the title of the magazine of the <a href="/wiki/Anarchism_in_Russia" title="Anarchism in Russia">Anarchist Communist Federation in Russia</a> around the time of the <a href="/wiki/Revolution_of_1905" class="mw-redirect" title="Revolution of 1905">1905 revolution</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Yaroslansky_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yaroslansky-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> and is still an imprint of the London group of the <a href="/wiki/Anarchist_Federation_(Britain_and_Ireland)" title="Anarchist Federation (Britain and Ireland)">Anarchist Federation (Britain and Ireland)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-afed_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-afed-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> To honour Gorky and his work, the name <i>Burevestnik</i> was bestowed on a variety of institutions, locations, and products in the USSR.<sup id="cite_ref-ziolkowski111_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ziolkowski111-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_storm_petrel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-80">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The 1903 edition of <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Dal" title="Vladimir Dal">Vladimir Dal</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Explanatory_Dictionary_of_the_Living_Great_Russian_Language" title="Explanatory Dictionary of the Living Great Russian Language">Explanatory Dictionary of the Living Great Russian Language</a></i>, defined <i>burevestnik</i> (the name of the bird actually used by Gorky) or "bird of storm" as a generic name for the <a href="/wiki/Procellariidae" title="Procellariidae">Procellariidae</a>, and illustrated it with several examples, including the species known in English as the <a href="/wiki/Wandering_albatross" title="Wandering albatross">wandering albatross</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_giant_petrel" title="Southern giant petrel">southern giant petrel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_fulmar" title="Northern fulmar">northern fulmar</a> and the European storm petrel. The Russian name for the European storm petrel, according to the same dictionary, is <i>kachurka</i>, rather than an <a href="/wiki/Adjective_phrase" title="Adjective phrase">adjective phrase</a> with <i>burevestnik</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dahl_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dahl-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> This is a modern reprint (using modernized Russian orthography) of the 1903 edition that would have been familiar to Gorky and his readers.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
